THE DUTY OF CITIZENS.
|.|i T ATIONAL patriotism, State pride and all the
1 \ thousand personal interests that are insepara
â¢' . bly bound up with the welfare of the nation
ifcad.'the State combine to impel citizens to the per
formance of their political duties, and yet, in spite
*>f all and to the neglect of all, a very large propor
tion of the people of this city have as yet failed to
Register as voters and thus assure their right to vote
fet- the coming elections.
There seems to be something like the deadness of
fipathy among a considerable number of citizens with
respect to politics. The registration at the City
Hall has been the lowest for many years, and but
three days remain for precinct registration. Unless
there is an immediate awakening of the laggards to
3 sense of their responsibilities as citizens it is cer
tain .that many will fail to register and thus by their
own fault forfeit the privilege of having a voice and
a vote in the election of Congressmen and State and
county officers this fall.
â¢ The worst feature of the situation is that it is al
most a certainty that those who have neglected to
register arc members of that class to which a com
munity has to look for support 1 against the machina
tions of bosses and the corrupting elements of poli
tics. . The henchmen of the bosses always register.
Their votes never fail at primaries, or on election
day. If. then, the citizens who are independent of
machine domination neglect to register, to attend
the primaries or to vote, the control of our politics
falls necessarily into the hands of the bosses and
those who arc willing to co-operate with them.
â¢ â  Three days remain for registration. The time has
come to make the last appeal to the laggards to
rouse up fn>ni their apathy and attend to their polit
ical duty. If they continue to neglect the trust the
Government reposed in them when it established
universal manhood suffrage they will surely regret it.
All citizens are concerned in the maintenance of
{good government and all should help to sustain it.
. ThjE CriEST-EXPANSION TEST.
DR. OLIX F. HARVEY of Pennsylvania, who
is reported to have recently examined more
than "a thousand applicants for admission to
the volunteer army, has embodied the results
pf his observations in a statement that the defects
jriost frequent among the applicants were those of
the eyes, of the teeth or of chest expansion.
Concerning defective teeth, or eyes, there are not
likely to be any great differences of opinion, but it
is questionable whether the chest expansion test is
fcfair one. According to reports of the statement
that have come to us, Dr. Harvey says: "Strange
jas.it may seem, nearly ail who had narrow chests
(were young farmers. Brought up to run a plow
tor hoe and scrape the ground with long-handled
tools, they had very strong arms and backs, but were
muscle bound and bent over. Theii- chests had been
contracted, and few of them could expand on inhala
tion the requisite two inches."
The very fact that young men brought up to work
ftn farms could not stand the chest expansion test is
one that tends to show there is something wrong
.with that standard of measuring strength or vital
ity. As a matter of fact, a great many people prac
tice abdominal breathing. Their chests are exer
cised only by labor. Therefore, the chest, while big
enough for the lungs and strong enough to enaMe
a man to work all day at a threshing machine, is not
elastic and does not respond easily to attempts at
â¢expanding it. On the other hand, some man who
has practiced chest breathing may have a great deal
of 'elasticity and expansion, and yet be but a weak
ling in comparison with the abdominal breather of
the harvest field.
â  Any set of muscles can be developed by exercise.
Continual piano playing will make a man's fingers
'supple and strong, but it would be absurd to assume
therefore that the hand of a piano player is stronger
than that of a farm boy who has been handling a
hayfork, and whose fingers are not nimble at all.
The elasticity of the client muscles and the conse
quent enlargement of the extent of chest expansion
arc undoubtedly desirable, but there is such a thing
as overrating their importance to the human organ
ism. " The greater portion of the Tungs are below
'the chest, and abdominal breathers are in most cases
possessed nf a larger lung power than the chest
breathers. There i.s many a gymnasium athlete who,
'if set to work in a harvest field, would get out of
wind, while the farm boy would be singing as he
worked, and much the same difference of condition
might result if the two were set to making quick
time on a long march.
As a general rule, it may be stated that any test
that excludes the American farmer's boy from the
ranks of a vitally strong manhood is very apt to be
wrong. It may appear that his chest is contracted
and his shoulders humped, according to city stand
ards, but he is nearly always of perdurable tough
ness, and whether at a fight or a frolic is a match
for any man he meets.
"JIM" REfTS TRIUMPH-
THE success which so far has attended in Santa
Clara County the efforts of James W. Rea to
defeat the Republican party of California this
year will be received by readers of The Call and Re
publicans generally, throughout the State, with re
gret and alarm.
Mr. Rea objected to an open primary altogether,
because such a thing would compel him to resort to
unfair methods for depriving the people of Santa
Clara County of representation in the State conven
tion. He much preferred to appoint the delegates,
and had he been let alone he would in so doing to a
great extent have concealed the nature of his politi
cal leadership. But the Republican State Central
Committee ordered a primary and Mr. Rea's follow
ers have been forced to disfranchise his constituents
by stuffing ballot boxes, suppressing legal votes, bull
doting respectable citizens and carrying things gen
erally with a high hand.
The affidavit of Charles J. Cornell, a resident of
San Jose, that one of Rea's heelers deliberately
pocketed his ballot and refused to deposit it in the
box typifies the methods of the boss. He has car
ried Santa Clara County by the most practical and
disreputable of political tactics.
But the questions which now demand an answer
are: Has Mr. Rea thereby won the votes of the
Republicans of that county? Can he, on election
day, secure their support for the ticket which the
men he has succeeded in sending to the Sacramento
convention will aid in nominating? Are these dele
gates to be received into the counsels of the State
Republican party with favor and will their opinions
with reference to platforms, policies and candidates
carry weight with the respectable Republicans who
must sit down with them at Sacramento on the 23d
inst, to choose a winning ticket?
The Republican party of California has begun
every campaign for many years with a bird of evil
omen perched above its head. "]\m" Rea, it now
seems probable, is to become the somber raven of
the campaign of 1808, unless events shall develop a
larger and more formidable imp in San Francisco.
We think it is time for the Republicans of Cali
fornia to note what every intelligent man in all par
ties now accepts as the truth, namely, that their party
cannot win so long as its Reas are permitted to con
trol its counsels. No stream ever rose above its
source. It will be impossible for Mr. Rea to nomi
nate a winning ticket. If his delegates trade or
combine with Pardee, Gage or any other candidate
that will kill him.
Xlie Republican Convention which
meet* at SacramentÂ© on the Â«3d inst.
must face the great Issue at last. Bo** and
corporation control muÂ»t be repudiated.
The people will never vote for candidates
which are the product of railroad com
binations and political bosslsm. This
fact is so conspicuous that It cannot l>e
gainsaid. The Republicans must cast out
their bosses and corruptionlsts or they
cannot hope to win this battle.
STUDYING THE LESSONS.
WAR is about over, at least so far as the West
Indies are concerned, and in that part of the
field we are now studying the lessons that
have been taught by the campaign. This, it is al
ready evident, is not going to be a pleasant task for
us, for despite the weakness of Spain she has given
us some things to learn that will seem bitter after the
glory in which we have been indulging.
The scope and intent of some of these lessons have
been made public even at this early stage of the
study. In the first place it has been shown that this
progressive nation actually sent to the field an army
equipped with out-of-date arms and ammunition, and
that the sluggish Spaniards were better supplied in
that respect. In the second place our facilities for
transporting troops, for feeding them, for equipping
them with arms and uniforms and for properly caring
for them when wounded, were all inadequate to the
requirements of the emergency.
The controversy now going on between the com
manding general at Santiago and the chief of the
medical staff as to the responsibility for the lack of
sufficient medical supplies at the front during the
fighting is one of the evidences that much has gone
wrong in the conduct of the campaign. Nor does
that controversy disclose the whole extent of it. Re
ports of other officers abound in statements of our
shortcomings. In fact it is beginning to appear that
our victorious march was due almost wholly to the
untiring energy and dauntless valor of our troops
rather than to any other superiority over the foe.
When we remember how proudly we have boasted
of our mechanical skill, our vast resources, our mas
tery of all the forces of science and art known to
civilization, and how often we have derided the
Spaniard for his medieval backwardness, it is dis
agreeable to learn that the Spanish outclassed us in
everything of that kind, and that we won simply be
cause we are the better fighting animal.
It is useless in prosecuting these studies to seek
to place the responsibility and the blame for our
shortcomings upon the shoulders of any particular
man or set of men. To do so would be to lose the
whole profit that we can otherwise derive from them.
The officers in command did the best they could, and
probably the best any one could, with the means at
their disposal. It is not their fault that the army had
old-fashioned rifles, no smokeless powder, no suffi
cient means for transporting artillery. Nor is it the
fault of the Government that adequate transports for
the troops were not at hand, that the army mail went
astray and that other evils happened.
The fault is in ourselves and not in our Govern
ment or in our officers. We have gone on in the
belief that this country has no need of an army. We
have permitted such nations as Spain and even
Turkey to surpass us in the efficiency of military
weapons. We have relied upon our isolation from
Europe to maintain peace and upon the inherent
valor of our people to win battles. This reliance has
not failed us utterly, but it has cost us much. We
ought therefore as a people learn the lessons of this
war and profit by them to such an extent that we will
not be caught so unprepared hereafter.
When Arkansas goes into the lynching business
there is nothing small about it. We are bound to say,
however, that the spectacle of five people, three of
them women, dangling from convenient limbs, is not
of a character to reflect credit upon American civili
zation.
It is a little early in the game to accuse Alger of
senrjing men to the front for political reasons. Of
course there are symptoms of peace, but they are
more apt to become impressive if there are plenty of
troops to encourage them.
News accounts speak of "busted" miners in the
Klondike. The expression may not be correct.
However, no better one has been suggested.
Aguinaldo and his followers are reported to be
sullen, but their state of mind is really a matter of
secondary importance.
THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, FRIDAY, AUGUST 12, 1898.
THE CRITICISM OF SOFTER.
YELLOW jealousy and yellow journalism com
bine to gird at General Shafter. He and his
old regiment are Californkins and our people,
regarding them as our fellow citizens, are interested
in their honor and welfare.
In the first place, General Shafter has been criti
cized for putting his men into battle so soon after
landing at Santiago. Every one familiar with that
tropical climate knows that, especially in the rainy
season, which was on when our army landed, men
from the temperate zone rapidly weaken and sicken.
Shafter was highly wise when he fought his men
as quickly as he could, for he had the use of the
strength they brought from the North, which ten
days later had been sapped completely by the tropi
cal heat and poison. There were many casualties.
Yes, but if the men had lain in camp for ten days
the Spaniards could have massacred them all, for
their strength would have been gone and they would
have been incapable of defense, to say nothing of
aggression. Again, the casualties were numerous
because it was the only real land battle fought (hir
ing the brief war. There have been gallant dashes
and heroic resistance to wild assaults, like that at
Malate, wherein the finest results of discipline and
the highest exhibitions of personal courage have
gratified the country, but at Santiago was the only
real, set battle of the whole war. There our troops
met an entrenched and stubborn foe. It was not a.
Spanish skedaddle, but a contest to the death on
both sides. It was a sustained action. Our troops
pressed forward step by step, and died to hold what
they had gained. Many fell, heroes all. and the
swart soldiers who fell under their fire were heroes
too. It was not an action with heroes on one side
and poltroons on the other.
Shafter and his council of war planned the battle
and it was fought out to the line on which they
laid it. The desperate attempt of Cervera to escape
and the destruction of his fleet hastened conclusions.
It is probable that, without that event, to have car
ried the city by assault would have required a con
siderable addition to our forces, fresh from the
North and bringing strength with it to replace that
which, was rapidly ebbing from the regiments first
landed.
General Shafter was not responsible for the lack
of equipment of the medical and hospital corps. He
was not responsible for the theft of what meager
stores there were by our ignoble allies, the thieves
tinder Garcia. Equipment is the business of the
quartermaster general and the surgeon general of
the army. Shafter is no more to blame for its lack
than he is for the poison climate of Cuba which
felled his men when Spanish bullets failed.
Shafter commanded in the single land battle and
won it. He received the surrender of a city and a
province and its guarding garrisons of 24,000 men.
He maintained civil order, set up a government and
promoted the resumption of commerce and the arts
of peace, so necessary to avert starvation of the
people. Carping and yellow criticism cannot alter
these facts nor tarnish his image in history.
OUR VANISHING FORESTS.
AT this season, when we are preparing to elect
a new Legislature, when progressive men are
considering what legislation to ask. on behalf
of the general welfare next winter, and when forest
fires are sweeping away thousands of dollars' worth
of property every week, it is pertinent both to the
politics and the industrial interests of the State to
iterate and reiterate the importance of providing pro
tection for our woodlands. For that reason, though
we have repeatedly directed attention to the subject
of late, we return to it this morning with a new argu
ment.
A few days ago we showed the interest taken by
the Eastern States in the problem of forest preserva
tion by extracts from a discussion upon the i>sue at
the recent meeting of the wood pulp paper manufac
turers. To-day we direct attention to the same prob
lem considered from the point of view of the Eastern
lumber industry. In reviewing the growing scarcity
of lumber woods and the increasing value of forests
as far west as Michigan and Wisconsin in a recent
issue the Timberman says:
"The greatest obstacle to a proper utilization of our
forest resources is that there are so many operating
lumbermen who arc carrying on their business, solely
for the present, with no regard for the future. They
should be brought to an appreciation of the fact, how
ever, that twenty years, or even ten years, is likely to
bring great changes in values of timber resources,
and therefore that within the life of the ordinary
lumber industry of to-day there is likely to be an in
creased value to the timber as it now stands which
will repay present care in its preservation."
According to that estimate, and there is no reason
to doubt its accuracy, the cost of forest preservation
in the East would be compensated for in twenty
years by the increase alone in the value of the timber
without counting anything of the gain of saving the
trees from destruction. If that is the condition of
affairs in the East that of the Pacific Coast cannot be
widely different. In our indifference to forest preserva
tion we are permitting the destruction of that one of
our natural resources whose comparative value is
most rapidly increasing with every year that passes.
If in the older States more serious and earnest
thought is given to this subject than with us, it is not
because the conditions there are widely different from
our own, or because the problem there is more im
portant, but solely because they have had a longer ex
perience of the evils of forest destruction and are now
suffering more acutely from the results of past negli
gence.
Not long ago the Philadelphia Record, in com
menting upon the waste of woods In that part of the
Union, said: "Within the past forty years vast tracts
of walnut, oak and other valuable forest trees have
been ruthlessly destroyed in this country, and some
varieties of these woods are now almost extinct." A
similar waste is going on in the great forests of the
Pacific Coast. It will not be long before we also shall
have to bewail past folly in this respect if we do not
profit by the experience of the older States and apply
at once the remedy of adequate protection to our
forests. Our woodlands are far-reaching, abundant
and prolific, but they are not inexhaustible, and the
question of their preservation is one of the most im
portant economic problems in the whole range of
practical legislation.
School Director Waller would be snfc even if some
one were really to shoot at him. It is believed by
those who saw him sprinting from imaginary danger
that he can outrun any bullet yet known to military
science.
Now the Spaniards are showing a disposition to
court-martial Toral, a circumstance proving that for
a Spanish general to exhibit any symptom of com
mon sense is the height of rashness.
We are hardly prepared to believe that having
polished us off Spain is preparing to try a round or
two with England.

SAN FRANCISCO, May 21. 1898.
Rev. Father Yorkeâ Editor Monitor:
The article in to-day's Monitor in which
my name is used in connection with the
Sailors' Home does me a gross injustice.
It is false, cowardly, contemptible, un
grateful, uncalled for and totally unde
served. C. - - CLINIUJN.
I do not know what ideas the honor
able gentleman may attach to tne
words used in that note, but to me it
seems a very serious attack to be called
an ingrate, a coward and a liar.
The second â¢'erroneous and mislead
ing" statement of which I am accused
is my charge against the Sailors' Home
The honorable Supervisor declares that
the Mayor and the Board of Supervi
sors thoroughly investigated the man
agement of the institution and found it
faultless. I beg to state that no inves
tigation, thorough or other, was made
by the Mayor and the Board of Super
visors. Such investigation to deserve
the name should have been made by
the board in the light of day and with
due notice to the persons concerned.
To merely hear the pleas of those in
terested in getting the lease, to listen
to the attorn- ys of the old society or
the officers thereof In a private room,
to stroll alone through the corridors
of the institution, these private and un
official actions do not constitute an in
vestigation.
Let me say h>rc, Mr. Editor, that the
facts which X have published about the
Sailors' Home have never been contro
verted. I have shown it to be a secta
rian institution. I have proved that it is
not a charitable institution. If the Hon.
Dr. Clinton, if his fellows on the Board
of Supervisors were acting in good
faith, why did they not meet these
charges? The facts arc backed up by
sufficient testimony. They have never
bt â¢i; explained away. No efficacious
means have been taken to prevent a
recurrence of them. The only answer
that has ever been proffered concern
ing tlu-m has been the Ipse dixit of Dr.
Clinton. The honorable doctor may be
a very great man, but he is not so great
that his mere denial will abolish facts.
That denial is of the less weight that it
is couched in such terms as "false, cow
ardly, contemptible, ungrateful, uncall
ed for and totally undeserved."
Let me tell Dr. Clinton that I am not
in the habit of making rash and reck
less assertions, and therefore I am not
to be turned from my purpose by rash
and reckless denials. I have made use
of many more opportunities than Dr.
Clinton has at his disposal to study
the question of the Sailors' Home. Were
I accustomed to deal in mere assertion,
to take rumor for truth, or to set forth
-itions that are not demonstra-
Ith certainty, I might have said
many more things about the Sailors'
Home, about the Board of Supervisors
and about the Hon. Dr. Clinton. I have,
v.-r, confined myself to hard facts
and they are sufficient to prove my con
tention.
The third "erroneous and misleading
Btatement" of which the honorable doc
â mplatns is my accusation that he
is the moving cause in this latest at
tack.
I have shown how much faith is to
li.- plared in his previous general de
nial, that he never attacked me, al
â  tly. Prom his own let
ter you may judge, Mr. Editor, how
much credence is to be placed in this
specific denial ay to this new attack.
It may be well, however, to remark
that ho attributes this late letter of the
Seamen's Friend Society t(. the fact
that: "In a recent number of the Mon
itor the subject was again brought up
and again the management of the Sail
ors' Home was attacked. This 'last

And faces panned gay
Go lurking round the rorridors

THE NEWSPAPER MAGAZINE.
The modern newspaper Is In many ways
a real magazine At its host it presents
illustrations of undisputed artistic ex
cellence, many of which tflve as good an
ldoa of the happening's thoty depict as
need be. A ttrst-r!:iss illustrator can catch
the spirit of an occurrence and portray it
as .satisfactorily as if it had been done
with a camera and published in the form
of a half-tone cut. Indeed, a pen-and-ink
â ketch is often superior to a photographic
reproduction. As for the literary quality
of the newspapers, In spite of all that has
been said derisively about newspaper
Efrigiish, the mass of the work of the
modern correspondent Is not so far below
that of the magazine contributors in
merit as to make comparison impossible.
Indeed, many of the best-known con
tributors to the monthly publications are
at the present moment engaged In work
straw' was pr.Wably the immediate
cause which moved the board, etc.,
etc.
The ingenuousness of this plea may
be judged by the fact that the manage
ment of the Sailors' Home was not at
tacked in a recent number of the Moni
tor. The only reference to the subject
made in a recent number of the Mom
tor is contained in this sentence: The
Supervisors who elected Reis .the in
dorsee of Apaism. and delivered over
the Sailors' Home to a sector an so
ciety, together -with Ragan a tail in the
School Board, are not fit men to be
nominated by any convention
This is an attack, not on the man
agement of the Sailors' Home, but on
fhe SuDervisors. The Supervisors,
therefore *Â£d he who was the most
guilty the Hon. Dr. Clinton, were the
Parties who needed defense. That de
fense was delivered from behind the
TiVttk-oats of the Ladies' Seamen s
end Society, and Dr. Clinton to the
contrary notwithstanding, was engi
neered by those whose political future
depended on the efficaciousness of the
Â£tt S me remind you, Mr. Editor, that
the following account of the proceed
ings appeared in your paper: exclaimed
"I offer as an amendment,' 1 exclaimed
Supervisor Clinton, "that the communi
caUon be spread in full on the minutes
of his meeting. I ask this in justice
to myself and to the other members of
the board who have been unjustly ac
cused The charges of the Monitor are
false and characterized by gross mis
representation. My character has been
attacked and I want to be placed right
on the record."
The Hon. Dr. Clinton was willing then
to stand sponsor for the bantling which
he would now disown. It was his
"apologia" and he would have it on
the records for all time. When three
months ago the management of the
Sailors' Home was criticized that man
agement did not dare reply even
though the uninitiated might have sup
posed that the lease was in jeopardy.
Now when the lease is secured the Sail
ors' Home is trotted out with an un
necessary justification of its actsâ and
why? Because Dr. Clinton is in danger.
When we remember this and bear in
mind the value of the former denials
of the honorable gentleman it requires
no gift of second sight to estimate at
its true value his disclaimer of connec
tion with the letter signed Nancy
Cooper and Anna McAnney.
For the honorable doctor's pious
prayers for my future welfare and con
version I am humbly grateful. As to
the value of such ostentatious suppli
cations you, Mr. Editor, may be doubt
ful when you remember the divine
1 warning, "And when ye pray, ye shall
not be as the hypocrites that love to
stand and pray in the synagogues and
corners of the streets that they may
be seen of men. Verily, I say unto you
they have received their reward." 1
sincerely hope the honorable gentleman
will not be defrauded of his due.
As for me I have no desire to quarrel
personally with him or to pay any at
tention to personal attacks on myself.
I have no recollection of having met
Dr. Clinton more than twice, once in a
street car and once on the occasion of
a sick call. Why, therefore, he should
claim to have been my friend passes
my comprehension; but, whether he
were friend or neighbor or even enemy
I have no desire to do him wrong. And
that I do him no wrong his manner of
action convinces me. When he meets
my facts with arguments other than
bare denials couched in abusive lan
guage and based on his unsupported
j say so I shall give him credit for good
! faith. Personal charges of untruth,
! cowardice and the like do not bother
me. I am afraid I have too many sins
of my own in the matter of personali
ties to answer for to be justified in re
plying to mere personal charges. But.
Mr. Editor, this is not a mere personal
matter. It is a great. question of prin
ciple â question of sectarianism or
non-sectarianism, for which thousands
of citizens of this city sacrificed party
and friends and office in order to set
tle it once for all in the sense of the
constitution. That this principle has
been betrayed by Dr. Clinton and by
his associates on the Board of Super
j visors is the sole conclusion deducible
' from the uncontroverted facts I have
placed before the public. Something is
; needed to offset these facts besides big
words, profuse professions and unctuous
! prayers. Yours truly,
i PETER C. TORKE.

OUR TREATMENT OF PRISONERS.
Good gunnery and dauntless valor nre
not the only features of our fighting that
have astonished the Spaniards. Th<\v ;ir>;
amazed at our courteous treatment of our
prisoners of war.
The Spanish press has contemptuously
characterized our soldiers and Bailors as
"American pigs." The Spanish editors
nave pictured our officers with all the
brutal attributes of barbarians. Hut
Spanish prisoners have found our officers
chivalrous and humane. They find that
a/nong the lirst concerns of an American
admiral or general after an engagement
are the safety and comfort of the pris
oners.
The conduct of the victors toward the 1
vanquished after the destruction of the
Spanish fleet at Santiago was magnani
mous In the extreme. Admiral Cervera
was more impressed by the courtesy of i
the American fleet officers and their en- ;
thuslastic congratulations than by any
other Incident connected with the crush
ing defeat. He was so elated over the
generous treatment accorded him that he
took especial care to acknowledge It in
his official dispatches to the Spanish au
thorities.
It is a pleasure to record the fact that
the American policy of dealing with pris
oners, was fully reciprocated in the chi
valrous treatment of Lieutenant Ho
and his crew 'of the Merrimac by both
Admiral Cervera and < '.â¢â â¢'.< â¢ r â¢ L i L,lnai
The Spanish officers at the front know
more than Spanish editors about the gal- i
laritry an( ' humanity of the American
soldier. They know that in battle he can
flsrht like a demon; that after the battle â 
he can act like a Christian.â Chicago i
Times-Herald.

THE VOLUNTEER ENGINEERS.
More than two hundred persons applied
for enlisting papers the other day at th&
recruiting office of the First Regiment,
United States Volunteer Engineers, 335
way. The engineer corps is to be
made up of electricians, linemen, steam
engineers, railroad men, track men, sur
veyors, lumberman, carpenters, masons,
sailors, boatmen, riggers, dock builders,
blasters, pipe calkers, blacksmiths,
wheelwrights, cooks, tailors and, shoe
makers.
"Such a regiment." said Adjutant
Biamwell, "will be entirely new to the
United States service. It is the plan fol
lowed in the European armies, and its ad
vantages will be at once apparent. Here
tofore the men in the engineer regiments
in service here were drilled and instructed
as miners and sappers, and their ability
ran chiefly to the construction of fortiil
cations. We want experts in thr^
rious lines, and our chief aim Is to have
each squad commanded by a sergeant
who knows how to work the men in these
various branches. Drilling and tactics
will be of secondary consideration, but
will receive sufficient attention. One> idea
is that if bridge building is required we
can at once call on one. or more squads
for the work, and so in every other
branch of work. It is my impression that
battalions of the regiment will be sent
into service soon after being sworn in,
and when it is completed the servi. â 
have the finest body of practical men
composing an engineer regiment that it
is possible to obtain."â New York Com
mercial Advertiser.

THE YOUNG MEN
ARE ORGANIZING
Eight of the eighteen clubs forming the
Young Men's Republican League have
now been organized, and three more will
be formed next week. On Monday night,
the young Republicans of the Thirty-sev
enth district will organize; on Tuesday
nicht "those of the Thirty-sixth, and on
Wednesday night the Fortieth district will
have a new club. In all these districts,
besides being active In club organisation,
the young Republicans are looking closely
after the matter of registration.

The.-: an- the delegates to the Demo
! cratic State Convention, elected at the
club primaries held in Alameda County
this afternoon and evening. There were
i no contests except In the Third and Sixth
war is in Oakland and these wore purely
local in their nature.
A majority are claimed for Magulre first
and last, although there are some who be
wlth W. \v. Foote, that it would be
better for the party to suffer temporary
Li than to submit to the dictation of
i the Populists in the matter of the guber
; natorial nomination. But if these dele
or a majority of themâ will support
the "Littlee Spotted <;iant." It is equally
certain that everj one of them will also
BUpport any plan that may be inaugurated
by the Interior delegations for the unseat
ing of the McNab sang from San Francis
co. It Is doubtful If there is a Democrat
in Alameda County who does not de
nounce the work of the political outlaws
across the bay, and If there is any possi
ble chance to overthrow them, Alameda
County partisans may be safely counted
on to assist in- the purification of the par
ty. Mast of the delegation will go to Sac
ramento Sunday night, ready to enter into
the thick of the fray.
In all the scheming and jobbing essen
tial to a ward politician conducting his
own campaign for Governor, Dr. Pardee,
as he stops to "spit" on his hands, pre
paratory to talcing a fresh "holt," finds
one thing to exult over, and, naturally .
he has to tell the world all about It. Like
a child with a stick of candy, all his sor
rows of yesterday are forgotten in his
newer Joy, and Dr. Pardee may now
justly claim that his fight is progressing
beyond his most ardent expectations.
To-night, with all the eclat of bold
faced type and (laming headlines. Dr.
Pardee announces to his admiring con
stituents that at last he has been able
to draw a crowd without the aid of either
a brass band or bonfire.
This is gratifying, indeed, for hereto-
It has been generally regarded as
essential that he have both accessories
in order to get anybody to listen to him.
Now that he has tried the experiment,
and found that freaks will call the cu
rious, even without flaring brass and blaz
ing tar barrels, Dr. Pardee. will probably
dismiss the half dozen musicians who
have nightly escorted him on his weary
rounds, while the Alameda small boy
will find a steady sourer' of revenue from
"hooking" fence boards and other com
bustible material cut off.
Let all rÂ«jolce with the doctor, for.
poor man, his enemies have got him
headed down a mighty thorny trail just
at present, and are goading him on to
such a pace that he tlnds little enough to
joy over.
STes, there Is one more source of com
fort to the doctor. In the same issue of
his organ that tells of his capabilities as
a museum attraction, Dr. Pardee finds
time and space to chortle mildly over his
fine work In the Bnclnal City in "stock
ing" the unpledged delegates, so called.
But this x:< ' la destined to end in woe,
for the Pardee tactics have thoroughly
aroused every Republican In the town,
and an opposition ticket will be in the
field, and not a nanio on it will be a Par
admirer.
The case of Alameda Is an Illustration
of the revulsion of sentiment that has
taken place in the past few weeks. When
Pardee first announced himself a candi
date, he had many warm friends over
there, who would have remained loyal
through thick and thin had it not been
for his arbitrary methods. But Dr. Par
eemed to regard Alameda as his by
right of Conquest and would not brook the
mention of Davis or any other aspirant
for the office. His friends grew restive
and the revolt came when Pardee impu
dently told thorn and his friends else
where that he would not trust them to
name delegates, hut would make up the
lists himself. The Alamedans declared
they would throw off the Pardee yoke and
name delegates, through their clubs, un
pledged to any man. but who would go
to Sacramento entirely free to work for
the best Interests of the county. By some
hook or crook, Pardee managed to .^neak
in six of his friends on this club lint, giv
ing three to Brown, thereby shutting Da
vis out entirely, besides throwing three
votes in the county to an outsider. Da
vis' friends were furious while the great
majority of Pardee's former admirers
openly denounced the job. They deter
mined to put up another ticket, and Deed'
less to say. no man svho is under the
slightest suspicion of being friendly to
.. will find his name on it. This
ticket will be elected beyond a doubt and
Pardee will Und to his cost that Ala
meda's nne delegates will be for Davia,
whereas had he kept his hands off ho
might have had them, should the eitu
ation at Sacramento show at any time
that they would do him any good.
This Is only one instance of how Pardee
is beating himself. Jn the Fourth Ward
in Oakland, where six weeks ago nobody
but Pardee was heard of, the shouts have
become stilled since the doctor baa given
out his little list with orders to his fol
lowers to vote for them blindly. There
are names on that list that good Repub
licans cannot and will not accept, and as
Hcans cannot and will not accept, and
as Pardee refuses to change them
there are rumors of discontent heard
now where formerly only joyous huzzas
rang out before. In the First, Third and
Sixth Wards the situation is about the
same as in the Fourth, while in the
Seventh, Pardee'a old home, the Davis
men are openly claiming they will carry
the ward. Berkeley and Washington and
Murray Townships are BOlid for Davis,
while Eden is debatable ground. Pardee'a
Bhouteraâ that Is the courthouse push,
which the doctor denounced nve years
ago as unclean and corruptâ still make
brave assertions that the doctor will get
anywhere up to 60 per cent of tho dele
gation, but it is painfully evident they
are whistling in the dark and that their
assertions merely voice their desires.
R. W. Snow is announced with a flour
ish as Pardee'S candidate for delegate
at-large from Alameda County. This
means that Mr. Snow has been promised
Pardee'a support in his light fur Mayor
of Oakland next spring.
J W. Nelson was the last man to re
ceive the doctor's doubtful support tor
the Mayoralty, and though he had the
indorsement of almost all the political
bodies, the Pardee handicap was bo great
that ho was badly beaten by J. L. !
who was only incumbered by ihe Populist
nomination.

COMMERCIAL WORLD.
SUMMARY OF THE MARKETS.
Silver about the same.
Wheat and Barley dull.
The other Cereals inactive.
Hay came In from Missouri.
Feed and MillstufCs unchanged.
White Beans firm and colored lower.
6weet Potatoes declined.
Tomatoes scarce and higher.
Turkeys continue to advance.
Butter, Cheese and Eggs about tha same.
Fruit Market glutted. '
Dried Fruit unchanged.
Large Government orders for Provisions.
Hides firm.
Hogs continue to weaken.
PLENTY OF GOLD.
THE COUNTRY SUKK < IF AN ABUNDANCE
OF THE Vi:l.I."W MKTAL.
The circular of Henry Clews, under date of
New York, August 6, says: "The Stock Mar
ket maintains a very strong undertone in spite
of limited activity. Peace prospects, of course,
strengthen values; but the i.pproachlng ces
sation of the war had relatively little effect.
The sltoatio.i Is, however, relieved of very
serious doubts; there Will be no further big
bond Issues, and the possibility of international
complications is more remote than at any
time since tha war began. Home conditions
axe unchanged. We have so frequently re
marked upon the improved agricultural. Indus-
trial and commercial condition of the country
that repetition is unnecessary. Nothing ex
cept unforeseen troubles can prevent a higher
slock market. At the moment the marki-t re
fuw B to move upward with any degree of force,
because the big leaders are not prepared to
take the Initiative. Many are away; and the
best leaders wisely prefer to wait until the
critical period of the crop situation is safHy
passed. Should no vigorous damage happen,
the upward movement Is likely to be vigor
ously renewed without opposition.
' An important factor In the situation will be
the large Increase of gold production. Accord
ing to the Mint Bureau at IVushington the j
World's product of gold In 1537 was JIi4O.UOO.iKK).
and the product #f IS9S Is estimated at $275. -
Â©OO,OOO, an increase of $35,000,000. Africa leads
as a gold producer, and is expected to turn
out J7J.ouo.ow this year. Australia and the
United .States are close competitors as gold
producers, each being expected to turn ont
about $60,000,000 this year. The Klondike Is
not proving of the importance anticipated.
The most careful estimates are for a product
of $10,000,000 in 1898, which is smaller than
either California or Colorado; allfornia's prod
uct this year being estimated at $17,000,000 and
Colorado's nt J1'3,000,000. Ten years ago the
â worlds gold product was (110,000,000. Since
then It has been more than doubled, and now
amounts, as Just said, to about $275,000,000.
This does not look like any scarcity of the
precious metal, especially considering the pros
: new supplies and the Increasing use of
other forms of money. Certainly there -will be
no scarcity of gold in the United States; for In
addition to our Increasing output and the
Klondike supply we are likely to have a large
influx of gold in settlement of our trade bal
ance. The latter can be settled by returning
our a<Â»curtties. which Is not likely; by an out
flow of American capital into foreign coun
tries, which may occur to some extent now that j
the war is over: or by the sending of gold, j
that being the most probable outcome. Evi
dently, then, we are sure of having an abund
ance of gold In the country. At the same time
the banks are anxious to expand their circula
tion as soon as they can secure sufficient of
the new 3 per cent bonds; so the prospects are
for an abundance of cheai> money for some
time to come, and- we need hardly Inform our
clients that this is a powerful aid to a rising ;
market."

EASTERN MARKETS.
NEW YORK STOCK MARKET.
NEW YORK. Aug. 11.â Today's business on
the Stock Exchange was the largest for many
months. Trading was very broad and compre- I
henslve and the tone of speculation extremely
varied. The general undertone continued un
usually strong. The decline in prices was not
allowed to proceed to any great length before
dullness ensued, and In a short time a renewed
demand would carry prices upward again, gen
erally on a much smaller volume of business
than on which they had declined. Business
was so large and so widely distributed that
movements of special stocks had little Influence
on the general list and the market at times
showed excessive Irregularity. Even while
large realizing was going on at one point an
eager demand would spring up at another and
keep the balance about even. Considering the
large total of the day's transactions net
changes are small. Orders placed with com
mission houses to buy stocks were large this
morning and more generally distributed than
at any time since the rise set in. This outside
demnnd was fed with a Steady stream of
stocks which obliterated the opening gain be
fore the end of the fir^t hour. A large part of
the outside orders especially In the grangers
were attributed to Western account. This buy
lag was induced by the unexpectedly favorable
ter of the Government crop report.
3ti-:ilizing was on a greater scale In Burlington
and Hock Island than In the other grangers,
jK-.ssibly due to the comparatively less favor
able showing of the corn crop. The advance In
Atchison preferred brought out such a flood of
offerings that the demand seemed to be.
abandoned largely and turned to other low
priced stocks. A number of preferred railroad
stocks which have not yet come to the full
dividends to which they are entitled and some
of the Junior stocks on which a surplus over
preferred stock has been earned but not dia
: were especially prominent. Northern
Pacific common was the leader In the late
speculation at an advance of 2 points, and
Denver preferred rose at one time 1%. The In
dustrial specialties were irregular. Sugar was
at one time above 140, while Manhattan fell as
low as lor. and was depressed all day.
There were heavy offerings of bonds at times,
especially of recently reorganized roads, a con
elderoble part of the selling being attributed to
foreign account. Total sales $4,320,000. United
Btatea new four? coupon declined % and the
threes when issued advanced l /i in the bid
price.
Total sales of stocks to-day 545,600 shares In
cluding 6100 Atchison, 40.500 Atchison preferred
8100 Che?, and Ohio, 32.50Q Burlington, 8600 c!

LONDON MARKET.
NEW YORK, Aug. 11.â The Evening Post's
financial cablegram says: The markets to-day
were quiet, but are steadily Improving. Jlr.
Curzon's appointment to India as Viceroy is
unanimously approved In the city. The feat
ure is the strength of Americans. The rise
was initiated by buying from New "York, but
there is also more business here than for some
time past. The public themselves are taking
a hand, although, as previously cabled, the
disposition In such quarters is to tak^ snort
profits Prices finished a trifle below the best
Grand Trunks were better. The half yearly
statement is expected immediately, and it Is
believed that full Interest will be paid on the
guaranteed stock with a lan?r amount carried
forward. Other markets were steady but feat
ureless. Gold was in strong demand at 77s

CHICAGO GRAIN MARKET.
CHICAGO, Aug. 11.â Instead of showing de
terioration in the condition of â¢ spring wheat
during the month, as expected, the Government
crop report made an Improvement of 1.5 points.
That caused the price of September to drop %c
under yesterday's close, opening trades being
at CsVi@Csc. After this expression of bearish
feeling, the market commenced to mend . a
little, the receipts at various Western markets
indicating that no Increase in the movement
had yet set In. The aggregate at the principal
primary markets was 380,500 bushels, against
807,000 bushels the year. before. This striking
deficiency, together with an eager demand for
shipping parcels, worked the price of Septem
ber up to 65% c. It was reported from Kansas
City that No. 2 hard was up 4c a bushel there,
and as there appeared a probability of another
decrease in the visible 'the market became very
strong after many changes of front. December
was depressed from beginning to end and
wound up %c lower for the day. â " September
closed Â£c higher.' ..'â '.â  â¢ , -
. â¢ Corn was influenced by the Government re
port, showing only 3.5 reduction in condition
after all that had been heard of serious dam
age. The recuperative buoyancy of wheat near
the close brought out some buying orders In
corn and the closing figures were the best of
the day. September closed \*@%c lower than
yesterday. . Oats followed the other grains,
closing unchanged to I^c down. Provisions
started weak with corn. A good cash demand
later, however, turned the market strong.
Pork left off unchanged, lard gained 2>Â£ig;sc
and ribs 2V4c.

DECIDUOUS AND CITRUS FRUITS.
There waj more fruit on the market yeater
day than on any other day this year. The
canners were still unable to relieve the glut
but expect to start again within a couple of
days. Green Bartlett Pears, which would keep
until next week, sold at the top quotation, but
all other stock dragged. Peacnes were unsal
able. Plums were lower.
There were plenty of Melons and Grapes on
the market, but prices keep up very well. Nec
tarine? are slow.
Lemons and Limes continue scarce and high.

THE STOCK MARKET.
Local securities were distinguished by activ
ity In Hawaiian Commercial, the stock falling
from $27 50 to $25 60. Hutchlnson advanced to
$54. The other securities ranged about the
same.
Mining stocks were dull and unchanged.
The Con. Cal. & Va. delinquent sale takes
place to-day.
The Pacific Vinegar and Pickle Works has
declared a dividend of 10c. and an extra one
of the same amount, payable on the 15th.

NEW TO CHICAGO.
A night scone in Chicago painted by the
Tribune of that town:
"Dressed all In bliick. clutching a great
bunch of roses in each hand, and bending
low over the handlebars of his bicycle, a
figure wildly Bcorched down one of tho
driveways of Lincoln Park on Thursday
night. A long, black pennant fluttt ed be
hind it.
" 'I>i<! yon sop it?' yelled the park police
men to each other. 'Did you see its tail?'
"They had. Some of the policemen grew
paie and commenced swearing off things.
"Policeman Bland sprinted acro-.s a
short cut to head off the black rider as
he rounded a curve. "Devil or man, I
commiviid you to stop.' shouted he, as he
caught the wheel and stopped it."
The black rider was arrest, d. He was a
man in evening dress, and hurrying to a
wedding. He was fined. As evening
clother; become more common in Chicago
the present fear of them there will die
away. â New York Sun.

the early morning tides are Riven In the left
hand column and the successive tides of the
day in the order of occurrence as to time The
second time column gives the second tide of

riven are addition* to the soundings on the

nnd ther the number given la subtracted from
"he depth given by the charts. The plane of
reference is the mean of the lower low waters.

TIME BALL.
Branch Hydrographic Office, U. S. N., Mer
chants' Exchange, San Francisco, August
11. 1898.
The time ball on the tower of the new Ferry
building was dropped at exactly noon to-day â
1. c., at noon of the 120 th meridian, or at 8
p m. Greenwich mean time.
CHAS. P. WELCH.
KnslÃn (retir^n. l\ S. X.. In charge.
NOTICE TO MARINEKS.
A branch of the T'nited States Hydrngraphlc
Office, located In the Merchants' Exchange, Is
maintained In San Francisco for the benefit of
mariners, without regard to nationality and
free of expense.
Navigators are cordially Invited to visit the
office, where complete sets of charts and Bail
ing directions of the world are kept on hand
for comparison and reference, and the latest
information can always ho obtained regarding
lights, dangers to navigation and all matters
of interest to ocean commerce.
The time ball on the tower of the new Ferry
building, at the foot of Market street, is hoisted
about ten minutes before noon and dropped at
no in. 120 th meridian, by telegraphic signal re
ceived each day from the Dnited States hlgnal
Observatory. Marc Island, Cal.
A notice stating whether the ball was droppea
on time or giving the error, If any. is published
In the morning papers the following day.
i 'HAS T WELCH.
Ensign (retire,]), i . S. N., in charge.

CHARGED WITH MURDER.
Etta M. Payne Swears ta a Complaint
Against Frank H. Cheesman.
OAKLAND, Aug. 11.â Miss Etta Payne,
one of the victim's of Frank H. .Chees
man's thirst for blood, and who is still
under treatment at the Receiving Hos
pital, swore to a complaint this morning,
charging Cheesman with the murder of
her stepfather, Henry J. Brooke. Chees
man will be arraigned before Judge Allen
to-morrow morning, and a date act for his
preliminary examination.
Cheesman was not present at the in
queÃt held last night, but when told by
the police officials what the verdict was
he expressed no surprise. The prisoner
still acts in a sullen manner, seemingly
caring little for the probable outcome of
his dastardly crime. He has not made
any outbreaks since his arrest, but pro
tests against watch being held by the two
men Captain Fletcher has placed over
him.
MELROSE POWDER WORKS.
The Supervisors Powerless to Pre
vent Their Reconstruction.
OAKLAND. Aug. 11.â It developed to
day at a meeting of the Board of Super
visors that the fuse works that caused
devastation, death and ruin at Melrose
may be rebuilt if the company so desires,
j the Supervisors having no prohibitive
powers in the matter.
A delegation of Fruitvale residents was
present at the meeting to protest against
the rcbuiding of the works of the West
ern Fuse and Explosive Company. The
Supervisors Individually expressed them
- aa opposed to the reconstruction
but declared that the board has no power
to prevent the company rebuilding.
Is. H. Marwedel, on behalf of the pro
testants, stated that President Lukens
had lost $75,000 in the explosion, which
gave an idea of the amount of powder
kept at the works, and he vainly hoped
that the Supervisors would pass an ordi
nance against a repetition of the recent
explosion. . , ,
â  of the residents present declared
that if the board could not give their dis
trict protection they would petition to
join the city of Oakland to enjoy munici
pal benefits.
District Attorney Snook .rendered an
(.pinion to the effect that the Supervisors
could not hinder a reconstruction.
It was finally decided that all parties in
terested in the matter meet next Tuesday
at 10 a. m. in the board's rooms. The
Judson Dynamite Powder Company, Mcl-
ETuse Works and Judson Chemical
Works will be requested to be represented
at this meeting.
REV. FATHER M'AVOY
PASSES FROM EARTH
END OF ONE OF THE OLDEST
PRIESTS IN THE STATE.
For Over Twenty Years in Charge of
the Parish at San Leandro and
San Lorenzo.
For over twenty years Father McAvoy
has been in charge of a parish in this
priests in the State, died this evening at
the Clergy House at San Leandro.
For over twenty years Father McAvoy
! has been in charge of a parish In this
i county. When he was first placed in
; charge his parish Included San Leandro,
I San Lorenzo, Mt. Eden, Elmhurst, Red
wood Canyon and Haywards, but as the
territory became settled he had to be re
lieved of a large portion of it. Lately
his parish has consisted of the towns of
j San Leandro and San Lorenzo. The priest
j has grown up with the neighborhood and
was known and beloved throughout the
large territory over which he served.
Father McAvoy has been in ill health
: for a long time, and only a week ago he
resigned and was to- take a long rest. He
w;us preparing to go away when his final
attack of sickness came.
During his pastorate Father McAvoy
has rendered faithful service to his
I church. He has built a convent at San
L*-andro aond a church at San Lorenzo. Ho
also was made the beneficiary of much
valuable property which, it Is said, he
willed to the Archbishop for the use of
the church. Father McAvoy was also ac
tive in civic work. He subscribed to and
was an active advocate of the cannery at
San Leandro. He saw that there were
many vacant houses in his parish and he
desired to fill thf-m and he never tlrfd of
making the argument that the most per
manent way to improve the town would
be to establish manufactures that would
provide work.

THE SA:Sr FRANCISCO CALL, FRIDAY, AUGUST 12, 1898.
SOCIETY HAS
TAKEN SIDES
Meeks' Chinese Cook Is
Not Without Friends.
SOLDIERS TO STAND TRIAL
WILL SHIMAN AND HARRY
BAKER MUST EXPLAIN.
Objected to Being Shot by a Kodak
and Are Charged With Break
ing a Chinaman's
Leg.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
908 Broadway, Aug. 11.
Society at San Ijcandro Is considerably
i disturbed over a preliminary examination
j for felony that Is to be held next Satur
â¢ day before Judge Qutnn. The prisoners
are Will Shiman and Harry Baker, and
they are charged with willfully and felon
iously attacking a Chinaman, to wit, the
cook of the Meek family, and breaking his
leg.
Will Shiman and Harry Baker come
from two of the best-known families In
the interior of the county. Mr. Shiman.
I Sr. is an old rancher who is credited with
j being comparatively wealthy. His family j
Is one of the leading ones of this county I
and William and Harry Baker are two
social lights. When the present war
broke out nothing would satisfy these
young men but to enlist in the Eighth
California Regiment, now stationed at
Camp Barrett. Lasjt Sundiiy the young
privates were granted a leave of absence
and went to San Lorenzo Junction on
their way home. While at the Junction
the Chinese cook came along on his wheel.
The Meek cook is a very intelligent, well
educated Chinaman. He is Americanized j
to a degree, even to the point of being a
kodak fiend. On the day mentioned the
cook went oiu with his kodak intending

OAKLAND HAS SCORED
AN IMPORTANT POINT
MAP SHOWING THE WATER
FRONT OF EARLY DAYS.
Evidence Introduced in the Famous
Suit That Carries With It Great
Weight.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
90S Broadway. Aug. 11.
Evidence was admltte 1 in the water
front case to-day that tw trs wry strongly
Dn the question of street openings through
the disputed territory In 1868. The admis
sion of the map in question Is a (Strong
point on behalf of the city of Oakland,
for it shows the dedication of certain
West Oakland streets and opens the way
to the other exhibits which establish the
city's claim to other streets. The exhibit
Is a copy of the one filed with the County
Recorder in IXC4, and bears the Indorse
ment of A. M. Church, then County Re
corder. It Is a plat of land kn><wn an
Oakland Point, at the old railroad ferry
landing, and describes certain land near
Willow street, along which the Alameda
local now runs.
Attorney A. A. Moore mule strenuous
objections against the introduction of the
map, and later, when it wms being used
as the basis for Engineer Sforgan's testi
mony, more objections were made. Judge
Ogden, however, ruled in favor of the city
on ail points.
The importance of this evidence is very
marked, because under the decision of the
Supreme Court the city of jOakhU d is en
titled to all streets through water front
property which hnd ever been dedicated
to public use. The map is said to prove
that several of these streets were dedi
cated prior to 1868.
W R Davis, who is making such a
strong fight on behalf of trie city, assorts
that Oakland will probably establish her
claim to streets opened to low-water mark
along that strip of territory extending
from Market street to the north arm of
the estuary. The city's attorneys also
hope to establish the city's right to Ado
line street and a number of streets in
West Oakland.

FOUGHT FOR
TWENTY YEARS
Ole Madsen Loses His
Wife and Home.
A NEAT LEGAL SKIRMISH
TROUBLE ALL CAUSED BY A
QUARREL OVER TITLE.
She Lost Her Property by Careless
ness and Regained It Through
Her Husband's Negli
gence.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
908 Broadway, Aug. 11.
Twenty-seven years of married life,
twenty of which according to the
testimony have been passed in fighting.
Very few complaints in the Alameda
courts contain such a history of alleged
Inhumanity as that of Caroline Madsen
against her husband.
Not only has Ole lost his wife, but ;
he has also lost that which he seemed
to prize a great deal more â the home at :
Eighteenth and Market streets. In the
recovery of his home a very interesting
legal battle was conducted. In the first
place Mrs. Madsen lost the property le- â 
gaily by allowing the statute to lapse. \
Then 010 lost the property legally by j
his attorneys failing to include In their
complaint the fact that the lapsing of
the statute had caused the property to
revert to him.
The two suits for divorce and for the
property hinge ontt on the other, and in
the latter more personality and more in- '
side history of their quarter of a cen- '
tury of married unhappiness came out !
than In the divorce stiit. The testimony
showed that soon after their marriage :
.Mrs. Madsen gave Ole $3000 with which
to buy a homo. After five years she
learned that the property stood in her :
husband's name. Then their trouble com- |
roenced. and they have been lighting j
since 1877 over the $3000 house that was;
bought with her money.
Mrs. Madsen intended to bring suit
to assert her personal right, but she
permitted her husband to induce her
to take no action und finally she found
that the time had passed in which she
could seek redress. Madsen filed hU suit
to quiet the title in himself, but in his
complaint he failed to show the main
point of his contention. Attorney Fry,
on beJfalf of Mrs. Madsen, objected to the ;
plaintiff bring allowed to amend his com
plaint a second time, and made a novel i
argument that as the woman had lost j
the property by her own carelessness
and had regained it through the care
lessness of her husband it would be in
consistent on the part of the court to
take sides. Judge Greene took the saracj
view, and granted the woman the prop- ,
erty as he had previously granted the
decree of divorce.

THE ARIZONA
TO BE READY

REVIEW OF SECOND BRIGADE
i
THE THREE REGIMENTS MAKE A
FINE SHOWING.
A Steamer Loaded With Provisions
and Supplies Will Be Sent
to General Merrftt at
Manila.
The transport steamer Arizona will be
ready for the reception of troops on Satur
day, 'and the order designating- the troops
that she Is to carry to Manila and statins
the time of embarkation will be issued to
day at military headquarters. The troops
'to go are two battalions of regularsâ one
from the Eighteenth and the other from
the Twenty-third Regiments of Infantryâ
and recruits for the Tenth Pennsylvania
and First Colorado.
Brigadier General Kins, In command of
the Second Brigade, will sail on the Ari
zona.
The transport Scandia, It la now stated,
will be ready to take on troops next
Thursday. She will carry the Seventh
California, some recruits and a portion
of the Hospital Corps. Her capacity is
1500 troops.
The Australia and City of Sydney are
expected back from Manila some time
next week, and will very likely he ordered
Immediately on a second trip to the Phil
ippines, followed by the City of Peking.
These three vessels will take the Fifty
first lowa, the Twentieth Kansas and
First Tennessee.
At an early day Major General Merriam
â will dispatch to Manila a steamer loaded
entirely with provisions for Major General
Merritt's forces. This supply will be suffi
cient to last 20,000 soldiers for several
months. These provisions are to be pur
chased in San Francisco.
SECOND BRIGADE REVIEW
Fifty- First lowa, First Tennes-
see and Twentieth Kansas
Make a Fine Showing.
Major General Merriam reviewed the
Second Brigade of the Philippine Island?
expeditionary forces at 10 o'clock yes
terday morning upon the golf grounds of
the Presidio reservation. Hundreds of
Interested people witnessed the review.
The Second Hripade was under the com
mand of Brigadier General Charles King,
aad that distinguished olucer has every |
right to feel proud of the men whom he j
has the honor to command, for the Fifty
first lowa, First Tennessee and Twen
tleth Kansas regiments made a showing
that has not been surpassed by any vol
teers now in the service.
In addition to Generals Merriam and
King and their respective staffs. General
Marcus P. Miller and staff were also
present. Lieutenant Colonel Stacpole of
the First New York, and a number of j
prominent National Guard officers like- j
â wise witnessed the review. First Lieu
tenant Floyd W. Harris, adjutant of the
Fourth United States Cavalry, and for
merly military attache for the Govern- '-
ment at the court of Belgium, was in the ;
reviewing party.
General Merriam was attended byTroop
B Fourth United States Cavalry, under
First Lieutenant Lockwood, in the ab- :
senco of First Lieutenant John M. Neall, ,
as an escort of honor, and his presence
was further recognized by a salute from !
the guns at Port Point. "When the gen- '
eral and staff arrived the regiments were :
formed In line of masses on the brow of
the hill facng Camp Merritt. with lowa
on the right, Tennessee in the center and !
Kansas on the left. The reviewing
party first rode around the brigade, after
which each regiment, headed by its band,
passed in review before Generals Mer
riam, Miller and King in columns of com
panies, and afterward in columns of
fours. ,
At the conclusion of the review Gener
als Merriam and Miller complimented
General King both on the appearance and
thÂ© condition of his brigade. The an
nouncement made to the brigade by Gen
eral King that it would be sent to Ma
nila as soon as the necessary transports
could be secured caused the wildest ex
citement, and the regiments cheered their
commanding officer to the echo.
TO ENTERTAIN THE SIXTH.
The Command Will Be Wel
comed by Native Daughters'
Red Cross Aid.
There will be a meeting to-night in Na
tive Sons' Hall of the Native Daughters'
Red Cross Association for the purpose of
perfecting arrangements for the enter
tainment to be given by that association
â¢to the members of the California Sixth
Regiment of Volunteers. It is proposed
to give a literary entertainment in the
fore part of the evening, and that will be
followed by a dance. There will be an
address of welcome to the soldiers by
Mips Eliza D. Keith of Alta Parlor, sec
retary of the association, and the re
sponse on behalf (if the Sixth California
will be by Major Grant.
The Exempt Fire Company of this clty
has donated the sum of $25 to the asso
ciation.
War Incidents.
Private Clarence Day, Company M.
"First Tennessee Regiment, died at tht IM
vlsion Hospital on Wednesday night of
typhoid fever. Five men of the regiment
â¢were sent back to their homes on yester
fiay on sick furloughs. In the future only
this number will be pent each day. The
men who went yesterday were furnished
â with the money necessary for their meals
upon the trip by the Bed Cross Society.
Sven Cappleton, acting hospital steward,
in charge of the nurses at the Presidio
camp. Is sick with the measles. Ife was
one of the nurses sent uere by Mrs.
Whitelaw Reid, and is a graduate of
Bellevm; Hospital, New York. Eugene S.
Wood, another of the acting hospital
steward*, is sick with malaria.

made in Troop B, Fourth Cavalry: Corpo
ral James Wilson to be sergeant, vice
Sergeant Brooke, appointed sergeant ma
jor .if the regiment, and Private Charles
Blackburn to be coirporal, vice Wilson,
promott d.

CRUELLY CRUSHED BY AN ENGINE.
Terrible Accident to Fred Buddms on the Shio
Himalaya,
Â® Fred Buddrus of 85 Madison avenue lost his life shortly after 1 o'clock Â®
Â® yesterday afternoon. He was engaged In running a donkey engine hoisting Â®
Â® freight on the Hawaiian ship Himalaya. He stopped the engine for the (â¢>
Â® purpose of adjusting some part of the machinery, and was standing on Â®
Â® the winding drum with a wrench "in his hand. His foot slipped and In try- Â®
Â® ing to recover his balance he started the engine going. In an instant he Â®
Â® was wound around the drum and the piston pounded his abdomen to a jelly, Â®
Â® killing him almost instar-tly. â¢ Â®
Â® Deputy Coroner McCormick was obliged to obtain the aid of several Â®
Â® engineers" before the mangled body of the unfortunate man could be taken Â®
Â® out. Â®
Â® The deceased was a native of Germany, 61 years old. and leaves a Â®
Â® widow and several stepdaughters. He had been out of work for two months Â®
Â® and secured employment last Tuesday from J. E. Whitney of 11 Steuart Â®
Â® street. Â®
MARINE FIREMEN WANT
NO COOLIE LABOR
TELEGRAMS SENT TO SECRETA
RIES ALGER AND LONG.
If No Reply Is Received the Trouble
Will Be Told to Senators Perkins
and White and Congress
man Maguire.
The Pacific Coast Marine Firemen
; Union has a hard fight on its hands. They
i want the Chinese crew of the Government
: transport Arizona replaced with white
: men. but Uncle Sam seems determined to
â  stick to the coolies. He has legislated
against them time and again and now
, when it comes to the simple manning of a
United States ship, paid for by the tax
payers of the country, the taxpayer has
to take a back seat and the coolie gets
j Secretary P. Rogers, In the name of the
! union, has sent the following telegram to
: Secretaries Alger and Long:
! -The Pacific Coast Marine Firemen
Union protests against the employment of
Chinese firemen on the steamer Arizona.

TOLD HER TO PRAY.
An Insane Man Vainly Attempted
to Kill His Stepmother.
William D. Outcalt, who was recently
discharged from the Insane asylum,
made a murderous attack on his Btep
motber last night. The latter lives at
171S Folsom street. Shortly after 7
o'clock Outcalt went home, and picking
up a hatchet told his stepmother to pray,
as he Intended to "chop her in pieces."
The father was In an adjoining room, and
realizing his wife's helplessness he hur
riedly summoned a policeman. The lat
ter reached the house just as Outcalt was
about to carry his threat into execu-
He was secured after a short struggle
and taken to the Receiving Hospital,
where h< was placed in a padded cell.
To-day an effort will be made to have
him sent back to the asylum.

HAS PASSED INTO
HIS ETERNAL SLEEP
CHARLES W. MANWARING AN
SWERS THE CALL OF DEATH.
His Demise Occurred at His Home
in This City Yesterday
Afternoon.
Charles W. Manwaring died at his
home, 375 l Seventeenth street, yesterday
afternoon at 5 o'clock. suffer
ing for the past six months from the ef
fects of a severe attack of pneumonia,
and though his life was despaired of by
his mother and friends, ho did not believe
that he would die. His hopefulness was
a characteristic of his business and po
litical life.
His faithful application to the interests
of his employers, J. Gundlacb & Co., and
earnest work In behaif of the Republican
County Ontral Committee, of which 'v
was chairman for several years, and
presidency of the Bear Club, won for him
the high esteem of the business men of
the city and the confidence of r;i-n en
gaged in political life. Hia petition for
appointment in the Internal Revenue De
partment was signed by a thousand busi
ness men, whose wealth represented
many millions of dollars.
Mis father, Charles C. Manwnrlng. was
a captain in the Civil War. and was killed
in the service of his country. l>> â 
v<as an only son anÂ«l made a home *oi
his mother, who is heartbroken with
grief at his death. Th<- funeral will bn
conducted by i'acitic Lodge, F. and A. M..
in which order be had taken the thirty
second degree. The burial will occur
probably on Sunday, though arrange
ments are not yet perfected. t
A BIG COURSING STAKE.
Over Eighty Fast Cogs Will Compete
at Ingleside Park To-Morrow.
A most enthusiastic gathering of the
leading leashmen of this city took place
last evening in Pythian Castle to enter
their charges and to witness the drawing
for the big meeting of eighty-four dogs
which will be held at Ingleside Park on
Saturday and Sunday. The park commit
tee did not desire more than a seventy
two dog stake, but the owners were so
deeply anxious to see their "flyem" In ac

in their favor. The draw was:

A School Girl Missing.
The disappearance of an 8-year-old school
girl from the residence of her parents, 735
Broadway, is causing much anxiety. The
child, I,upe Saucedo. Is the Btepdaugh
ter of Secundino Soto. She attended
stepdaughter of Seeundino Soto. She
the Broadway Grammar School on Mon
day last. On her return from school at
3 o'clock pho went out on the street to
play. Since then she has not been seen.
)l< r parents have made diligent Beach for
her but so far nothing- has been ascer
tained as to her whereabouts.

SHIPS FOR THE
SOUND TRADE
Improved Steamer Serv
ice to Northern Ports.
TRIED TO END HER LIFE
A COLORED WOMAN ATTEMPTS
TO JUMP OVEBBOARD.
Jules Hansen, an ex-Sailor and Now
a Soldier, Causes Trouble on
the Water Front â Notes
Here and There.
There is to be a semi-weekly steamer
Bervice between San Francisco und Puget
Sound ports in the near future. The 3'a
clric Coast Steamship Company has been
contemplating the change for some time,
but the opening of the war and the con-
Bequent withdrawal of the steamers Sen
ator and City of Puebla delayed the mat
ter. The steamer Corona will Ije rÂ» ;,<s>â¢ for
service In a few days, however, and as
soon as she is put In commission the new
schedule will commence.
The Corona, it will be remembered, ran
ashore while on her way from Dyea and
Skaguay, but she was afterward got off
the beach and patched up. Temporary re
pairs were made at Seattle and the
steamer came to San Francisco and has
been overhauled at the Union Iron Works.
When completed she will go on the south
ern run, taking: the place of the Santa
Itosa. The latter vessel will be put on
the Puget Sound route and will run in
conjunction with the Umatllla, Walla
Walla and Queen. Steamers will leave
San Francisco Wednesday and Saturday
of each week and will arrive at Victoria,
B. C. each Monday and Friday and Seat
tle every Tuesday and Saturday. Return
ing the steamers will leave Seattle every
Sunday and Thursday, arriving here ev
ery Wednesday and Sunday. The return
of the Senator and City of Pueblo, will
make a change in the vessels of the Sound
Beet, but it will make no change In the
sailing days. The increase in the passen
ger and freight business between San
Francisco and Puget Sound ports has
made the addition of another vessel to the
fleet an solute necessity.
The schooner Hattie Phillips, which
brought the Blackburn party of Klondik
ers from Gloucester, Mass., has been sold
to W. Loanza & Co., for Mexican parties.
The vessel is at present in Oakland Creek,
but she is to be pulled out to-day and
overhauled, after which she will be put
under the Mexican flag. The Phillips will
be engaged in trade on the Mexican coast.
The British bark West York will get
away for Sydney, X. S. W., to-day. The
trouble over her deckload of lumber has
been adjusted. The captain of the bark
was willing to allow 25.000 feet of lumber
on the deck, but the charterers wanted to
put double that quantity aboard, so the
matter ended In none at all being taken.
The members of the State Typograph
ical Union are to make an excursion
around the bay to-morrow. They have
secured the tug Governor Markham from
the Harbor Commissioners and will leavo
Mission-street wharf at 9 a. m. All
points of interest on the bay will be vis
ited. *.
The river steamer Zinfandel is kept very
busy these days. Napa County Is sending
out more products than any other place
in the State, and nearly all of it is coming
down by steamer. Yesterday the Zinfan
del brought down 22,000 gallons of win*
and a large consignment of hay, beside*
quantities of fruit. The Zinfandel trans
ers her hay to other river steamers and
most of It goes up the San Joaqutn and
Sacramento.
Jules Hansen, an ex-sailor, but now a
volunteer soldier, made considerable trou
ble on the front yesterday. He raised a
disturbance at Main and Harrison, and
Special Officer Fitzgerald had a great
time In securing him. He finally got him
tied up with some hay rope, and sent
to the Harbor Receiving Hospital for the
ambulance. At the hospital Hansen ran
amuck as soon as his bonds were severed.
He seized a chair and began chasing Drs.
Hill, Green and Zabala around the operat
ing table. Finally one of the doctors
tripped the crazy follow up, and in a few
minutes he was strapped to a cot. All
that could be rot out of him was that
his name was Jules Hansen, and that he
was a soldier, but had been a sailor.
The United States steamer Albatross
Bailed for Guatemala at 2 p. m. yester
day. She Will StOP at Acapulco for (â ..:â !
and will then hurry to San Jose. The
chances are that the rebellion in Guate
mala will be over by the time she reaches
Ban Jose. The departure Of the Alba- :
tross leaves Lieutenant J. 08. Gunn in
temporary command of the mosquito
fleet, and he is, in consequence, the proud
est man afloat. He will be relieved of his
authority when the gunboat Bennington
comes down from Mare Island.
Edith Quinn (or, Sadie Stanley), a col
ored woman, attempted to commit suicide
from the ferry steamer Piedmont yester
day morning. Charles Nelson, one of the
deckhands, saw her and frustrated the at
tempt. When the Piedmont landed her
passengers on this side Miss Quinn was
turned over to Sergeant Mahoney. who
took her to the Harbor Police station.
She promised Captain Dunleavy that she
would not again attempt to commit sui
cide, so he let her go, but told Officer
Charles Callahan to keep a lookout for
her. Soon after this Callahan saw her
at Clay-street wharf, getting ready to
jump into the bay again. He put her un
der arrest, and acting under instructions j
from Captain Dunleavy took her to the
new City Hall, where *he will be exam
ined by the Insanity Commissioners. Miss
Quinn has a brother working on the
steamer Newark.
Captain Tom Lockyer came back to San
Francisco from Seattle on the bark Ger
mania yesterday. Since he left here In
command of the steamer "Whltelaw, Tom ,
has been in a peck of trouble. He was
bound for Dyea and Skaguay with Klon
dikers, and the passengers rose in revolt
over the provisions, and mode him put I
into Port Angeles. There was more trou- '<
ble at Skaguay. and to crown all, the
"W'hitelaw caught fire and was burned to
the water's edge. Lockyer lost every
thing he had in the world in the blaze and
has been In hard luck ever since. Tom is
a first-class navigator and one of the best
known tugboat men in California. He has
a host of friends, and all of them sym
pathize with him in this ill turn of for
tune's wheel
